Chapter I

Down the Rabbit-Hole

Alice was beginning to get very tired of sitting by her sister on the bank, and of having nothing to do: once or twice she had peeped into the book her sister was reading, but it had no pictures or conversations in it, 聭and what is the use of a book,聮 thought Alice 聭without pictures or conversation?聮

So she was considering in her own mind (as well as she could, for the hot day made her feel very sleepy and stupid), whether the pleasure of making a daisychain would be worth the trouble of getting up and picking the daisies, when suddenly a White Rabbit with pink eyes ran close by her.

There was nothing so VERY remarkable in that; nor did Alice think it so VERY much out of the way to hear the Rabbit say to itself, 聭Oh dear! Oh dear! I shall be late!聮 (when she thought it over afterwards, it occurred to her that she ought to have wondered at this, but at the time it all seemed quite natural); but when the Rabbit actually TOOK A WATCH OUT OF ITS WAISTCOATPOCKET, and looked at it, and then hurried on, Alice started to her feet, for it flashed across her mind that she had never before seen a rabbit with either a waistcoatpocket, or a watch to take out of it, and burning with curiosity, she ran across the field after it, and fortunately was just in time to see it pop down a large rabbithole under the hedge.

In another moment down went Alice after it, never once considering how in the world she was to get out again.

The rabbithole went straight on like a tunnel for some way, and then dipped suddenly down, so suddenly that Alice had not a moment to think about stopping herself before she found herself falling down a very deep well.

Either the well was very deep, or she fell very slowly, for she had plenty of time as she went down to look about her and to wonder what was going to happen next. First, she tried to look down and make out what she was coming to, but it was too dark to see anything; then she looked at the sides of the well, and noticed that they were filled with cupboards and bookshelves; here and there she saw maps and pictures hung upon pegs. She took down a jar from one of the shelves as she passed; it was labelled 聭ORANGE MARMALADE聮[2 - ORANGE MARMALADE 聳 矛脿冒矛氓毛脿盲 聳 媒貌卯 芒脿冒氓铆眉氓, 茂冒猫茫卯貌卯芒毛氓铆铆卯氓 猫莽 枚猫貌冒贸帽卯芒没玫 (卯谩没梅铆卯 猫莽 脿茂氓毛眉帽猫铆卯芒).], but to her great disappointment it was empty: she did not like to drop the jar for fear of killing somebody, so managed to put it into one of the cupboards as she fell past it.

聭Well!聮 thought Alice to herself, 聭after such a fall as this, I shall think nothing of tumbling down stairs! How brave they聮ll all think me at home! Why, I wouldn聮t say anything about it, even if I fell off the top of the house!聮 (Which was very likely true.)

Down, down, down. Would the fall NEVER come to an end! 聭I wonder how many miles[3 - Miles 聳 矛猫毛猫, 谩冒猫貌脿铆帽锚猫氓 氓盲猫铆猫枚没 猫莽矛氓冒氓铆猫每 冒脿帽帽貌卯每铆猫每. 1 矛猫毛每 = 1609 锚矛.] I聮ve fallen by this time?聮 she said aloud. 聭I must be getting somewhere near the centre of the earth. Presently she began again. 聭I wonder if I shall fall right THROUGH the earth! How funny it聮ll seem to come out among the people that walk with their heads downward! Do you think you could manage it?) 聭And what an ignorant little girl she聮ll think me for asking! No, it聮ll never do to ask: perhaps I shall see it written up somewhere.聮

Down, down, down. There was nothing else to do, so Alice soon began talking again. 聭Dinah聮ll miss me very much tonight, I should think!聮 (Dinah was the cat.) 聭I hope they聮ll remember her saucer of milk at teatime. Dinah my dear! I wish you were down here with me! There are no mice in the air, I聮m afraid, but you might catch a bat, and that聮s very like a mouse, you know. But do cats eat bats, I wonder?聮 And here Alice began to get rather sleepy, and went on saying to herself, in a dreamy sort of way, 聭Do cats eat bats? Do cats eat bats?聮 and sometimes, 聭Do bats eat cats?聮 for, you see, as she couldn聮t answer either question, it didn聮t much matter which way she put it. She felt that she was dozing off, and had just begun to dream that she was walking hand in hand with Dinah, and saying to her very earnestly, 聭Now, Dinah, tell me the truth: did you ever eat a bat?聮 when suddenly, thump! thump! down she came upon a heap of sticks and dry leaves, and the fall was over.

Alice was not a bit hurt, and she jumped up on to her feet in a moment: she looked up, but it was all dark overhead; before her was another long passage, and the White Rabbit was still in sight, hurrying down it. There was not a moment to be lost: away went Alice like the wind, and was just in time to hear it say, as it turned a corner, 聭Oh my ears and whiskers, how late it聮s getting!聮 She was close behind it when she turned the corner, but the Rabbit was no longer to be seen: she found herself in a long, low hall, which was lit up by a row of lamps hanging from the roof.

There were doors all round the hall, but they were all locked; and when Alice had been all the way down one side and up the other, trying every door, she walked sadly down the middle, wondering how she was ever to get out again.

Suddenly she came upon a little threelegged table, all made of solid glass; there was nothing on it except a tiny golden key, and Alice聮s first thought was that it might belong to one of the doors of the hall; but, alas! either the locks were too large, or the key was too small, but at any rate it would not open any of them. However, on the second time round, she came upon a low curtain she had not noticed before, and behind it was a little door about fifteen inches[5 - Inch 聳 盲镁茅矛, 氓盲猫铆猫枚脿 猫莽矛氓冒氓铆猫每 盲毛猫铆没. 1 盲镁茅矛 聳 2,54 帽矛.] high: she tried the little golden key in the lock, and to her great delight it fitted!

Alice opened the door and found that it led into a small passage, not much larger than a rathole: she knelt down and looked along the passage into the loveliest garden you ever saw. How she longed to get out of that dark hall, and wander about among those beds of bright flowers and those cool fountains, but she could not even get her head though the doorway; 聭and even if my head would go through,聮 thought poor Alice, 聭it would be of very little use without my shoulders. Oh, how I wish I could shut up like a telescope! I think I could, if I only know how to begin.聮 For, you see, so many outoftheway things had happened lately, that Alice had begun to think that very few things indeed were really impossible.

There seemed to be no use in waiting by the little door, so she went back to the table, half hoping she might find another key on it, or at any rate a book of rules for shutting people up like telescopes: this time she found a little bottle on it, (聭which certainly was not here before,聮 said Alice,) and round the neck of the bottle was a paper label, with the words 聭DRINK ME聮 beautifully printed on it in large letters.

It was all very well to say 聭Drink me,聮 but the wise little Alice was not going to do THAT in a hurry. 聭No, I聮ll look first,聮 she said, 聭and see whether it聮s marked 聯poison聰 or not聮; for she had read several nice little histories about children who had got burnt, and eaten up by wild beasts and other unpleasant things, all because they WOULD not remember the simple rules their friends had taught them: such as, that a redhot poker will burn you if you hold it too long; and that if you cut your finger VERY deeply with a knife, it usually bleeds; and she had never forgotten that, if you drink much from a bottle marked 聭poison,聮 it is almost certain to disagree with you, sooner or later.

However, this bottle was NOT marked 聭poison,聮 so Alice ventured to taste it, and finding it very nice, (it had, in fact, a sort of mixed flavour of cherrytart, custard, pineapple, roast turkey, toffee, and hot buttered toast,) she very soon finished it off.


* * *

聭What a curious feeling!聮 said Alice; 聭I must be shutting up like a telescope.聮

And so it was indeed: she was now only ten inches high, and her face brightened up at the thought that she was now the right size for going through the little door into that lovely garden. First, however, she waited for a few minutes to see if she was going to shrink any further: she felt a little nervous about this; 聭for it might end, you know,聮 said Alice to herself, 聭in my going out altogether, like a candle. I wonder what I should be like then?聮 And she tried to fancy what the flame of a candle is like after the candle is blown out, for she could not remember ever having seen such a thing.

After a while, finding that nothing more happened, she decided on going into the garden at once; but, alas for poor Alice! when she got to the door, she found she had forgotten the little golden key, and when she went back to the table for it, she found she could not possibly reach it: she could see it quite plainly through the glass, and she tried her best to climb up one of the legs of the table, but it was too slippery; and when she had tired herself out with trying, the poor little thing sat down and cried.

聭Come, there聮s no use in crying like that!聮 said Alice to herself, rather sharply; 聭I advise you to leave off this minute!聮 She generally gave herself very good advice, (though she very seldom followed it), and sometimes she scolded herself so severely as to bring tears into her eyes; and once she remembered trying to box her own ears for having cheated herself in a game of croquet she was playing against herself, for this curious child was very fond of pretending to be two people. 聭But it聮s no use now,聮 thought poor Alice, 聭to pretend to be two people! Why, there聮s hardly enough of me left to make ONE respectable person!聮

Soon her eye fell on a little glass box that was lying under the table: she opened it, and found in it a very small cake, on which the words 聭EAT ME聮 were beautifully marked in currants[6 - 脩urrants 聳 帽贸酶氓铆没茅 芒猫铆卯茫冒脿盲 帽卯冒貌脿 芦锚卯冒猫铆锚脿禄 聳 矛氓毛锚猫茅, 帽毛脿盲锚猫茅 猫 谩氓莽 锚卯帽貌卯梅氓锚. 脜茫卯 梅脿帽貌卯 锚毛脿盲贸貌 芒 芒没茂氓梅锚贸.]. 聭Well, I聮ll eat it,聮 said Alice, 聭and if it makes me grow larger, I can reach the key; and if it makes me grow smaller, I can creep under the door; so either way I聮ll get into the garden, and I don聮t care which happens!聮

She ate a little bit, and said anxiously to herself, 聭Which way? Which way?聮, holding her hand on the top of her head to feel which way it was growing, and she was quite surprised to find that she remained the same size: to be sure, this generally happens when one eats cake, but Alice had got so much into the way of expecting nothing but outoftheway things to happen, that it seemed quite dull and stupid for life to go on in the common way.

Chapter IV

The Rabbit Sends in a Little Bill

It was the White Rabbit, trotting slowly back again, and looking anxiously about as it went, as if it had lost something; and she heard it muttering to itself 聭The Duchess! The Duchess! By this time she had found her way into a tidy little room with a table in the window, and on it (as she had hoped) a fan and two or three pairs of tiny white kid gloves: she took up the fan and a pair of the gloves, and was just going to leave the room, when her eye fell upon a little bottle that stood near the lookingglass. So she swallowed one of the cakes, and was delighted to find that she began shrinking directly. As soon as she was small enough to get through the door, she ran out of the house, and found quite a crowd of little animals and birds waiting outside. The poor little Lizard, Bill, was in the middle, being held up by two guineapigs, who were giving it something out of a bottle. They all made a rush at Alice the moment she appeared; but she ran off as hard as she could, and soon found herself safe in a thick wood.

聭The first thing I聮ve got to do,聮 said Alice to herself, as she wandered about in the wood, 聭is to grow to my right size again; and the second thing is to find my way into that lovely garden. I think that will be the best plan.聮

It sounded an excellent plan, no doubt, and very neatly and simply arranged; the only difficulty was, that she had not the smallest idea how to set about it; and while she was peering about anxiously among the trees, a little sharp bark just over her head made her look up in a great hurry.

An enormous puppy was looking down at her with large round eyes, and feebly stretching out one paw, trying to touch her. 聭Poor little thing!聮 said Alice, in a coaxing tone, and she tried hard to whistle to it; but she was terribly frightened all the time at the thought that it might be hungry, in which case it would be very likely to eat her up in spite of all her coaxing.

Hardly knowing what she did, she picked up a little bit of stick, and held it out to the puppy; whereupon the puppy jumped into the air off all its feet at once, with a yelp of delight, and rushed at the stick, and made believe to worry it; then Alice dodged behind a great thistle, to keep herself from being run over; and the moment she appeared on the other side, the puppy made another rush at the stick, and tumbled head over heels in its hurry to get hold of it; then Alice, thinking it was very like having a game of play with a carthorse, and expecting every moment to be trampled under its feet, ran round the thistle again; then the puppy began a series of short charges at the stick, running a very little way forwards each time and a long way back, and barking hoarsely all the while, till at last it sat down a good way off, panting, with its tongue hanging out of its mouth, and its great eyes half shut.

This seemed to Alice a good opportunity for making her escape; so she set off at once, and ran till she was quite tired and out of breath, and till the puppy聮s bark sounded quite faint in the distance.

聭And yet what a dear little puppy it was!聮 said Alice, as she leant against a buttercup to rest herself, and fanned herself with one of the leaves: 聭I should have liked teaching it tricks very much, if 聳 if I聮d only been the right size to do it! Oh dear! I聮d nearly forgotten that I聮ve got to grow up again! Let me see 聳 how IS it to be managed? I suppose I ought to eat or drink something or other; but the great question is, what?聮

The great question certainly was, what? Alice looked all round her at the flowers and the blades of grass, but she did not see anything that looked like the right thing to eat or drink under the circumstances. There was a large mushroom growing near her, about the same height as herself; and when she had looked under it, and on both sides of it, and behind it, it occurred to her that she might as well look and see what was on the top of it.

She stretched herself up on tiptoe, and peeped over the edge of the mushroom, and her eyes immediately met those of a large caterpillar, that was sitting on the top with its arms folded, quietly smoking a long hookah, and taking not the smallest notice of her or of anything else.



Chapter V

Advice from a Caterpillar

The Caterpillar and Alice looked at each other for some time in silence: at last the Caterpillar took the hookah out of its mouth, and addressed her in a languid, sleepy voice.

聭Who are YOU?聮 said the Caterpillar.

This was not an encouraging opening for a conversation. Alice replied, rather shyly, 聭I 聳 I hardly know, sir, just at present 聳 at least I know who I WAS when I got up this morning, but I think I must have been changed several times since then.聮

聭What do you mean by that?聮 said the Caterpillar sternly. 聭Explain yourself!聮

聭I can聮t explain MYSELF, I聮m afraid, sir聮 said Alice, 聭because I聮m not myself, you see.聮

聭Come back!聮 the Caterpillar called after her. 聭I聮ve something important to say!聮

However, at last she stretched her arms round it as far as they would go, and broke off a bit of the edge with each hand.

聭And now which is which?聮 she said to herself, and nibbled a little of the righthand bit to try the effect: the next moment she felt a violent blow underneath her chin: it had struck her foot!

She was a good deal frightened by this very sudden change, but she felt that there was no time to be lost, as she was shrinking rapidly; so she set to work at once to eat some of the other bit. Her chin was pressed so closely against her foot, that there was hardly room to open her mouth; but she did it at last, and managed to swallow a morsel of the lefthand bit.


* * *

聭Come, my head聮s free at last!聮 said Alice in a tone of delight, which changed into alarm in another moment, when she found that her shoulders were nowhere to be found: all she could see, when she looked down, was an immense length of neck, which seemed to rise like a stalk out of a sea of green leaves that lay far below her.

聭What CAN all that green stuff be?聮 said Alice. 聭And where HAVE my shoulders got to? And oh, my poor hands, how is it I can聮t see you?聮 She was moving them about as she spoke, but no result seemed to follow, except a little shaking among the distant green leaves.

As there seemed to be no chance of getting her hands up to her head, she tried to get her head down to them, and was delighted to find that her neck would bend about easily in any direction, like a serpent. She had just succeeded in curving it down into a graceful zigzag, and was going to dive in among the leaves, which she found to be nothing but the tops of the trees under which she had been wandering, when a sharp hiss made her draw back in a hurry: a large pigeon had flown into her face, and was beating her violently with its wings.

聭Serpent!聮 screamed the Pigeon.

聭I聮m NOT a serpent!聮 said Alice indignantly. 聭Let me alone!聮

聭Serpent, I say again!聮 repeated the Pigeon, but in a more subdued tone, and added with a kind of sob, 聭I聮ve tried every way, and nothing seems to suit them!聮

Alice was more and more puzzled, but she thought there was no use in saying anything more till the Pigeon had finished.

聭As if it wasn聮t trouble enough hatching the eggs,聮 said the Pigeon; 聭but I must be on the lookout for serpents night and day! Why, I haven聮t had a wink of sleep these three weeks!聮

聭I HAVE tasted eggs, certainly,聮 said Alice, who was a very truthful child; 聭but little girls eat eggs quite as much as serpents do, you know.聮

This was such a new idea to Alice, that she was quite silent for a minute or two, which gave the Pigeon the opportunity of adding, 聭You聮re looking for eggs, I know THAT well enough; and what does it matter to me whether you聮re a little girl or a serpent?聮

聭It matters a good deal to ME,聮 said Alice hastily; 聭but I聮m not looking for eggs, as it happens; and if I was, I shouldn聮t want YOURS: I don聮t like them raw.聮

聭Well, be off, then!聮 said the Pigeon in a sulky tone, as it settled down again into its nest. Alice crouched down among the trees as well as she could, for her neck kept getting entangled among the branches, and every now and then she had to stop and untwist it. After a while she remembered that she still held the pieces of mushroom in her hands, and she set to work very carefully, nibbling first at one and then at the other, and growing sometimes taller and sometimes shorter, until she had succeeded in bringing herself down to her usual height.

It was so long since she had been anything near the right size, that it felt quite strange at first; but she got used to it in a few minutes, and began talking to herself, as usual. 聭Come, there聮s half my plan done now! Chapter VI

Pig and Pepper

For a minute or two she stood looking at the house, and wondering what to do next, when suddenly a footman in livery came running out of the wood 聳 (she considered him to be a footman because he was in livery: otherwise, judging by his face only, she would have called him a fish) 聳 and rapped loudly at the door with his knuckles. It was opened by another footman in livery, with a round face, and large eyes like a frog; and both footmen, Alice noticed, had powdered hair that curled all over their heads. She felt very curious to know what it was all about, and crept a little way out of the wood to listen.

The FishFootman began by producing from under his arm a great letter, nearly as large as himself, and this he handed over to the other, saying, in a solemn tone, 聭For the Duchess. Chapter VII

A Mad Tea-Party

There was a table set out under a tree in front of the house, and the March Hare and the Hatter were having tea at it: a Dormouse was sitting between them, fast asleep, and the other two were using it as a cushion, resting their elbows on it, and talking over its head. 聭Very uncomfortable for the Dormouse,聮 thought Alice; 聭only, as it聮s asleep, I suppose it doesn聮t mind.聮

The table was a large one, but the three were all crowded together at one corner of it: 聭No room! No room!聮 they cried out when they saw Alice coming. 聭There聮s PLENTY of room!聮 said Alice indignantly, and she sat down in a large armchair at one end of the table.

Chapter XI

Who Stole the Tarts?

The King and Queen of Hearts were seated on their throne when they arrived, with a great crowd assembled about them 聳 all sorts of little birds and beasts, as well as the whole pack of cards: the Knave was standing before them, in chains, with a soldier on each side to guard him; and near the King was the White Rabbit, with a trumpet in one hand, and a scroll of parchment in the other. In the very middle of the court was a table, with a large dish of tarts upon it: they looked so good, that it made Alice quite hungry to look at them 聳 聭I wish they聮d get the trial done,聮 she thought, 聭and hand round the refreshments!聮 But there seemed to be no chance of this, so she began looking at everything about her, to pass away the time.

Alice had never been in a court of justice before, but she had read about them in books, and she was quite pleased to find that she knew the name of nearly everything there. 聭That聮s the judge,聮 she said to herself, 聭because of his great wig.聮

The judge, by the way, was the King; and as he wore his crown over the wig, (look at the frontispiece if you want to see how he did it,) he did not look at all comfortable, and it was certainly not becoming.

聭And that聮s the jurybox,聮 thought Alice, 聭and those twelve creatures,聮 (she was obliged to say 聭creatures,聮 you see, because some of them were animals, and some were birds,) 聭I suppose they are the jurors.聮 She said this last word two or three times over to herself, being rather proud of it: for she thought, and rightly too, that very few little girls of her age knew the meaning of it at all. However, 聭jurymen聮 would have done just as well.

The twelve jurors were all writing very busily on slates. 聭What are they doing?聮 Alice whispered to the Gryphon. 聭They can聮t have anything to put down yet, before the trial聮s begun.聮

聭They聮re putting down their names,聮 the Gryphon whispered in reply, 聭for fear they should forget them before the end of the trial.聮

聭Stupid things!聮 Alice began in a loud, indignant voice, but she stopped hastily, for the White Rabbit cried out, 聭Silence in the court!聮 and the King put on his spectacles and looked anxiously round, to make out who was talking.

Alice could see, as well as if she were looking over their shoulders, that all the jurors were writing down 聭stupid things!聮 on their slates, and she could even make out that one of them didn聮t know how to spell 聭stupid,聮 and that he had to ask his neighbour to tell him. 聭A nice muddle their slates聮ll be in before the trial聮s over!聮 thought Alice.

One of the jurors had a pencil that squeaked. This of course, Alice could not stand, and she went round the court and got behind him, and very soon found an opportunity of taking it away. She did it so quickly that the poor little juror (it was Bill, the Lizard) could not make out at all what had become of it; so, after hunting all about for it, he was obliged to write with one finger for the rest of the day; and this was of very little use, as it left no mark on the slate.

聭Herald, read the accusation!聮 said the King.

On this the White Rabbit blew three blasts on the trumpet, and then unrolled the parchment scroll, and read as follows: 聳

The first witness was the Hatter. He came in with a teacup in one hand and a piece of breadandbutter in the other. 聭I beg pardon, your Majesty,聮 he began, 聭for bringing these in: but I hadn聮t quite finished my tea when I was sent for.聮

Just at this moment Alice felt a very curious sensation, which puzzled her a good deal until she made out what it was: she was beginning to grow larger again, and she thought at first she would get up and leave the court; but on second thoughts she decided to remain where she was as long as there was room for her.

Alice watched the White Rabbit as he fumbled over the list, feeling very curious to see what the next witness would be like, 聭聳for they haven聮t got much evidence YET,聮 she said to herself. Chapter XII

Alice聮s Evidence

聭Here!聮 cried Alice, quite forgetting in the flurry of the moment how large she had grown in the last few minutes, and she jumped up in such a hurry that she tipped over the jurybox with the edge of her skirt, upsetting all the jurymen on to the heads of the crowd below, and there they lay sprawling about, reminding her very much of a globe of goldfish she had accidentally upset the week before.

聭Oh, I BEG your pardon!聮 she exclaimed in a tone of great dismay, and began picking them up again as quickly as she could, for the accident of the goldfish kept running in her head, and she had a vague sort of idea that they must be collected at once and put back into the jurybox, or they would die.

聭The trial cannot proceed,聮 said the King in a very grave voice, 聭until all the jurymen are back in their proper places 聳 ALL,聮 he repeated with great emphasis, looking hard at Alice as he said do.

Alice looked at the jurybox, and saw that, in her haste, she had put the Lizard in head downwards, and the poor little thing was waving its tail about in a melancholy way, being quite unable to move. She soon got it out again, and put it right; 聭not that it signifies much,聮 she said to herself; 聭I should think it would be QUITE as much use in the trial one way up as the other.聮

As soon as the jury had a little recovered from the shock of being upset, and their slates and pencils had been found and handed back to them, they set to work very diligently to write out a history of the accident, all except the Lizard, who seemed too much overcome to do anything but sit with its mouth open, gazing up into the roof of the court.

聭All right, so far,聮 said the King, and he went on muttering over the verses to himself: 聭聯WE KNOW IT TO BE TRUE聳聰 that聮s the jury, of course 聳 聯I GAVE HER ONE, THEY GAVE HIM TWO聳聰 why, that must be what he did with the tarts, you know聳聮

聭Why, there they are!聮 said the King triumphantly, pointing to the tarts on the table. 聭Nothing can be clearer than THAT. Then again 聳 聯BEFORE SHE HAD THIS FIT聳聰 you never had fits, my dear, I think?聮 he said to the Queen.

聭Never!聮 said the Queen furiously, throwing an inkstand at the Lizard as she spoke. (The unfortunate little Bill had left off writing on his slate with one finger, as he found it made no mark; but he now hastily began again, using the ink, that was trickling down his face, as long as it lasted.)

聭Then the words don聮t FIT you,聮 said the King, looking round the court with a smile. There was a dead silence.

聭It聮s a pun!聮 the King added in an offended tone, and everybody laughed, 聭Let the jury consider their verdict,聮 the King said, for about the twentieth time that day.

聭No, no!聮 said the Queen. 聭Sentence first 聳 verdict afterwards.聮

聭Stuff and nonsense!聮 said Alice loudly. 聭The idea of having the sentence first!聮

聭Hold your tongue!聮 said the Queen, turning purple.

聭I won聮t!聮 said Alice.

聭Off with her head!聮 the Queen shouted at the top of her voice. Nobody moved.

聭Who cares for you?聮 said Alice, (she had grown to her full size by this time.) 聭You聮re nothing but a pack of cards!聮

At this the whole pack rose up into the air, and came flying down upon her: she gave a little scream, half of fright and half of anger, and tried to beat them off, and found herself lying on the bank, with her head in the lap of her sister, who was gently brushing away some dead leaves that had fluttered down from the trees upon her face.

聭Wake up, Alice dear!聮 said her sister; 聭Why, what a long sleep you聮ve had!聮

聭Oh, I聮ve had such a curious dream!聮 said Alice, and she told her sister, as well as she could remember them, all these strange Adventures of hers that you have just been reading about; and when she had finished, her sister kissed her, and said, 聭It WAS a curious dream, dear, certainly: but now run in to your tea; it聮s getting late.聮 So Alice got up and ran off, thinking while she ran, as well she might, what a wonderful dream it had been.

But her sister sat still just as she left her, leaning her head on her hand, watching the setting sun, and thinking of little Alice and all her wonderful Adventures, till she too began dreaming after a fashion, and this was her dream: 聳

First, she dreamed of little Alice herself, and once again the tiny hands were clasped upon her knee, and the bright eager eyes were looking up into hers聳she could hear the very tones of her voice, and see that queer little toss of her head to keep back the wandering hair that WOULD always get into her eyes 聳 and still as she listened, or seemed to listen, the whole place around her became alive the strange creatures of her little sister聮s dream.

The long grass rustled at her feet as the White Rabbit hurried by 聳 the frightened Mouse splashed his way through the neighbouring pool 聳 she could hear the rattle of the teacups as the March Hare and his friends shared their neverending meal, and the shrill voice of the Queen ordering off her unfortunate guests to execution 聳 once more the pigbaby was sneezing on the Duchess聮s knee, while plates and dishes crashed around it 聳 once more the shriek of the Gryphon, the squeaking of the Lizard聮s slatepencil, and the choking of the suppressed guineapigs, filled the air, mixed up with the distant sobs of the miserable Mock Turtle.

So she sat on, with closed eyes, and half believed herself in Wonderland, though she knew she had but to open them again, and all would change to dull reality 聳 the grass would be only rustling in the wind, and the pool rippling to the waving of the reeds 聳 the rattling teacups would change to tinkling sheepbells, and the Queen聮s shrill cries to the voice of the shepherd boy 聳 and the sneeze of the baby, the shriek of the Gryphon, and all thy other queer noises, would change (she knew) to the confused clamour of the busy farmyard 聳 while the lowing of the cattle in the distance would take the place of the Mock Turtle聮s heavy sobs.

Lastly, she pictured to herself how this same little sister of hers would, in the aftertime, be herself a grown woman; and how she would keep, through all her riper years, the simple and loving heart of her childhood: and how she would gather about her other little children, and make THEIR eyes bright and eager with many a strange tale, perhaps even with the dream of Wonderland of long ago: and how she would feel with all their simple sorrows, and find a pleasure in all their simple joys, remembering her own childlife, and the happy summer days.



Through The Looking-Glass



White Pawn (Alice) to play, and win in eleven moves




DRAMATIC PERSON&#198; (as arranged before commencement of game)



		Child of the pure unclouded brow
		And dreaming eyes of wonder!
		Though time be fleet, and I and thou

		Thy loving smile will surely hail

		No thought of me shall find a place

		A tale begun in other days,

		A simple chime, that served to time

		A melancholy maiden!
		We are but older children, dear,
		Who fret to find our bedtime near.
		Without, the frost, the blinding snow,
		The stormwind聮s moody madness 聳

		And childhood聮s nest of gladness.
		The magic words shall hold thee fast:
		Thou shalt not heed the raving blast.
		And, though the shadow of a sigh
		May tremble through the story,
		For 聭happy summer days聮 gone by,
		And vanish聮d summer glory 聳

		The pleasance of our fairytale.

Looking-Glass House

One thing was certain, that the white kitten had had nothing to do with it 聳 it was the black kitten聮s fault entirely. 聭It seems very pretty,聮 she said when she had finished it, 聭but it聮s rather hard to understand!聮 (You see she didn聮t like to confess, even to herself, that she couldn聮t make it out at all.) 聭Somehow it seems to fill my head with ideas 聳 only I don聮t exactly know what they are! Chapter II

The Garden of Live Flowers

聭I should see the garden far better,聮 said Alice to herself, 聭if I could get to the top of that hill: and here聮s a path that leads straight to it 聳 at least, no, it doesn聮t do that 聳聮 (after going a few yards along the path, and turning several sharp corners), 聭but I suppose it will at last. Chapter III

Looking-Glass Insects

Of course the first thing to do was to make a grand survey of the country she was going to travel through. 聭It聮s something very like learning geography,聮 thought Alice, as she stood on tiptoe in hopes of being able to see a little further. 聭Principal rivers 聳 there are none. However, this was anything but a regular bee: in fact, it was an elephant 聳 as Alice soon found out, though the idea quite took her breath away at first. 聭And what enormous flowers they must be!聮 was her next idea. 聭Something like cottages with the roofs taken off, and stalks put to them 聳 and what quantities of honey they must make! 聭I think I聮ll go down the other way,聮 she said after a pause; 聭and perhaps I may visit the elephants later on. Besides, I do so want to get into the Third Square!聮

So, with this excuse, she ran down the hill and jumped over the first of the six little brooks.


* * *

聭Tickets, please!聮 said the Guard, putting his head in at the window. In a moment everybody was holding out a ticket: they were about the same size as the people, and quite seemed to fill the carriage.

聭Now then! Show your ticket, child!聮 the Guard went on, looking angrily at Alice. And a great many voices all said together (聭like the chorus of a song,聮 thought Alice), 聭Don聮t keep him waiting, child! Why, his time is worth a thousand pounds a minute!聮

聭Don聮t make excuses,聮 said the Guard: 聭you should have bought one from the enginedriver.聮 And once more the chorus of voices went on with 聭The man that drives the engine. Why, the smoke alone is worth a thousand pounds a puff!聮

Alice thought to herself, 聭Then there聮s no use in speaking.聮 The voices didn聮t join in this time, as she hadn聮t spoken, but, to her great surprise, they all thought in chorus (I hope you understand what thinking in chorus means 聳 for I must confess that I don聮t), 聭Better say nothing at all. 聭I shall dream about a thousand pounds tonight, I know I shall!聮 thought Alice.

Chapter V

Wool and Water

She caught the shawl as she spoke, and looked about for the owner: in another moment the White Queen came running wildly through the wood, with both arms stretched out wide, as if she were flying, and Alice very civilly went to meet her with the shawl.

The next moment soldiers came running through the wood, at first in twos and threes, then ten or twenty together, and at last in such crowds that they seemed to fill the whole forest. Alice got behind a tree, for fear of being run over, and watched them go by.

She thought that in all her life she had never seen soldiers so uncertain on their feet: they were always tripping over something or other, and whenever one went down, several more always fell over him, so that the ground was soon covered with little heaps of men.

Then came the horses. Having four feet, these managed rather better than the footsoldiers: but even they stumbled now and then; and it seemed to be a regular rule that, whenever a horse stumbled, the rider fell off instantly. Chapter IX

Queen Alice

I聮m sure your paw can wait!聮 But the provoking kitten only began on the other paw, and pretended it hadn聮t heard the question.

Which do you think it was?

		A boat beneath a sunny sky,[99 - A boat beneath a sunny sky 聳 媒貌卯 脿锚冒卯帽貌猫玫: 茂氓冒芒没氓 谩贸锚芒没 帽貌冒卯锚 帽锚毛脿盲没芒脿镁貌帽每 芒 猫矛每 脌毛猫帽没 聳 Alice Pleasance Liddell.]
		Lingering onward dreamily
		In an evening of July 聳

		Children three that nestle near,
		Eager eye and willing ear,
		Pleased a simple tale to hear 聳

		Long had paled that sunny sky:
		Echoes fade and memories die:
		Autumn frosts have slain July.

		Still she haunts me, phantomwise,
		Alice moving under skies
		Never seen by waking eyes.

		Children yet, the tale to hear,
		Eager eye and willing ear,
		Lovingly shall nestle near.

		In a Wonderland they lie,
		Dreaming as the days go by,
		Dreaming as the summers die:

		Ever drifting down the stream 聳
		Lingering in the golden gleam 聳
		Life, what is it but a dream?

脧卯 毛氓茫氓铆盲氓, 脳脿冒毛眉莽 脣镁貌芒猫盲忙 脛卯盲忙帽卯铆 (谩卯毛氓氓 猫莽芒氓帽貌铆没茅 茂卯盲 茂帽氓芒盲卯铆猫矛卯矛 脣眉镁猫帽 脢媒冒冒卯毛毛) 帽卯梅猫铆猫毛 猫帽貌卯冒猫镁 卯 茂冒猫锚毛镁梅氓铆猫每玫 脌毛猫帽没 芒 脩貌冒脿铆氓 梅贸盲氓帽 芒卯 芒冒氓矛每 锚脿貌脿铆猫每 铆脿 毛卯盲锚氓 帽 茂冒氓茂卯盲卯谩铆没矛 脨卯谩猫铆帽卯铆卯矛 脛脿锚贸卯冒貌卯矛 猫 貌冒氓矛每 帽氓帽貌冒脿矛猫 脣猫盲盲氓毛毛 聳 脣卯冒猫铆卯茅, 脌毛猫帽卯茅 猫 脻盲猫貌. 脗茂卯帽毛氓盲帽貌芒猫猫 猫帽貌卯冒猫每 谩没毛脿 铆氓矛铆卯茫卯 猫莽矛氓铆氓铆脿 猫 卯茂贸谩毛猫锚卯芒脿铆脿.

To the LookingGlass world it was Alice that said聟 聳 媒貌卯 茂脿冒卯盲猫每 铆脿 帽貌猫玫卯貌芒卯冒氓铆猫氓 脗脿毛眉貌氓冒脿 脩锚卯貌貌脿 芦脢冒脿帽脿芒梅猫锚 脛脿铆盲猫禄 (1825), 茂卯帽芒每霉氓铆铆卯氓 茫氓冒卯镁 每锚卯谩猫貌帽锚猫玫 芒卯茅铆 脛忙卯铆贸 脙冒媒玫氓矛贸 脢毛媒芒氓冒玫脿贸莽贸, 茂氓冒芒卯矛贸 芒猫锚卯铆貌贸 脛脿铆盲猫.

脥脿 卯谩氓盲脿玫 谩毛镁盲脿 茂卯盲脿芒脿毛猫帽眉 芒 卯茂冒氓盲氓毛氓铆铆卯茅 茂卯帽毛氓盲卯芒脿貌氓毛眉铆卯帽貌猫: 莽脿锚贸帽锚猫 (氓帽毛猫 谩没毛猫), 帽贸茂, 冒没谩脿, 盲猫梅眉, 忙脿冒锚卯氓 猫 盲氓帽氓冒貌. 脌毛猫帽脿 茂冒卯茂贸帽貌猫毛脿 茂氓冒芒贸镁 茂卯毛卯芒猫铆贸 卯谩氓盲脿.



96

脻貌卯 铆氓 茂冒卯帽貌卯 卯梅氓冒氓盲铆卯氓 帽貌猫玫卯貌芒卯冒氓铆猫氓 卯 冒没谩氓: 毛镁谩猫貌氓毛猫 脣眉镁猫帽脿 脢媒冒冒卯毛毛脿 芒没梅猫帽毛猫毛猫, 梅貌卯 冒脿莽茫脿盲锚脿 聳 贸帽貌冒猫枚脿!



97

Extinguisher 聳 矛氓貌脿毛毛猫梅氓帽锚猫茅 锚卯毛茂脿梅卯锚 铆脿 盲毛猫铆铆卯茅 冒贸梅锚氓, 帽 茂卯矛卯霉眉镁 锚卯貌卯冒卯茫卯 茫脿帽猫毛猫 帽芒氓梅猫. 脗 帽卯芒冒氓矛氓铆铆卯矛 每莽没锚氓 extinguisher 卯莽铆脿梅脿氓貌 卯茫铆氓貌贸酶猫貌氓毛眉.



98

Decanters 聳 芒猫铆铆没氓 茫冒脿么猫铆没.



99

A boat beneath a sunny sky 聳 媒貌卯 脿锚冒卯帽貌猫玫: 茂氓冒芒没氓 谩贸锚芒没 帽貌冒卯锚 帽锚毛脿盲没芒脿镁貌帽每 芒 猫矛每 脌毛猫帽没 聳 Alice Pleasance Liddell.
